        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714"><meta property="og:title" content="Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/acsnano.7b08714.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Two-dimensional (2D) antimonene is a promising anode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theoretical capacity of 660 mAh g–1 and enlarged surface active sites. However, its Na storage properties and sodiation/desodiation mechanism have not been fully explored. Herein, we propose the sodiation/desodiation reaction mechanism of 2D few-layer antimonene (FLA) based on results acquired by in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction, ex situ selected-area electron diffraction, and theoretical simulations. Our study shows that the FLA undergoes anisotropic volume expansion along the a/b plane and exhibits reversible crystalline phase evolution (Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na3Sb) during cycling. Density-functional theory calculations demonstrate that the FLA has a small Na-ion diffusion barrier of 0.14 eV. The FLA delivers a larger capacity of 642 mAh g–1 at 0.1 C (1 C = 660 mA g–1) and a high rate capability of 429 mAh g–1 at 5 C and maintains a stable capacity of 620 mA g–1 at 0.5 C with 99.7% capacity retention from the 10th to the 150th cycle. Considering the 660 mAh g–1 theoretical capacity of Sb, the electrochemical utilization of Sb atoms of FLA is as high as 93.9% at a rate of 0.5 C for over 150 cycles, which is the highest capacity and Sb utilization ratio reported so far. Our study discloses the Na storage mechanism of 2D FLA, boosting promising applications of 2D materials for advanced SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/acsnano.7b08714.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ee43ed25775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ee43ed25775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'f5e08b20-e93e-4e78-a3e4-a5820843203b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6ee43ed25775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ee43ed25775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

1887-1893</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="ancac3" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1738870756703.json"><div <script nonce="90f6ee43ed25775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Few-Layer Antimonene: Anisotropic Expansion and Reversible Crystalline-Phase Evolution Enable Large-Capacity and Long-Life Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.7b08714", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D1887%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWeifeng%2BTian%252C%2BShengli%2BZhang%252C%2BChengxue%2BHuo%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D2%26contentID%3Dacsnano.7b08714%26title%3DFew-Layer%2BAntimonene%253A%2BAnisotropic%2BExpansion%2Band%2BReversible%2BCrystalline-Phase%2BEvolution%2BEnable%2BLarge-Capacity%2Band%2BLong-Life%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D1893%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2018" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Two-dimensional (2D) antimonene is a promising anode material in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theoretical capacity of 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and enlarged surface active sites. However, its Na storage properties and sodiation/desodiation mechanism have not been fully explored. Herein, we propose the sodiation/desodiation reaction mechanism of 2D few-layer antimonene (FLA) based on results acquired by <i>in situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction, <i>ex situ</i> selected-area electron diffraction, and theoretical simulations. Our study shows that the FLA undergoes anisotropic volume expansion along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> plane and exhibits reversible crystalline phase evolution (Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) during cycling. Density-functional theory calculations demonstrate that the FLA has a small Na-ion diffusion barrier of 0.14 eV. The FLA delivers a larger capacity of 642 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C = 660 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and a high rate capability of 429 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 C and maintains a stable capacity of 620 mA g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C with 99.7% capacity retention from the 10th to the 150th cycle. Considering the 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> theoretical capacity of Sb, the electrochemical utilization of Sb atoms of FLA is as high as 93.9% at a rate of 0.5 C for over 150 cycles, which is the highest capacity and Sb utilization ratio reported so far. Our study discloses the Na storage mechanism of 2D FLA, boosting promising applications of 2D materials for advanced SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D1887%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWeifeng%2BTian%252C%2BShengli%2BZhang%252C%2BChengxue%2BHuo%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D2%26contentID%3Dacsnano.7b08714%26title%3DFew-Layer%2BAntimonene%253A%2BAnisotropic%2BExpansion%2Band%2BReversible%2BCrystalline-Phase%2BEvolution%2BEnable%2BLarge-Capacity%2Band%2BLong-Life%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D1893%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291146&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Lattices" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Lattices" class="article__tags__link">Lattices</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Few-layer+Antimonene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anisotropic+Expansion&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anisotropic%20expansion">anisotropic expansion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reversible+Crystalline-phase+Evolution&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reversible%20crystalline-phase%20evolution">reversible crystalline-phase evolution</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Large+Capacity&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_large%20capacity">large capacity</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div id="ac_i1" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h2 class="article-section__title section__title" id="_i1"> </h2><div class="NLM_p">The large demand for portable electronics, electronic vehicles, and large-scale power grid storage has spurred the development of advanced energy storage technologies and systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Among the various energy storage devices, room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted increasing attention as alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) because sodium (Na) is abundant, economical, and readily available worldwide. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Since the performance of SIBs significantly depends on the Na storage capability of electrode materials, developing promising electrode materials with rational structures and surface properties is of great importance to promote the performance of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Inspired by the large-capacity alloy anodes in LIBs, alloy-based anode materials for Na storage have attracted much attention lately. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5, 6)</a> In particular, antimony (Sb) is an appealing anode material for SIBs because of its large theoretical capacity of 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and low discharge potential of about 0.5 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Unfortunately, bulk Sb generally shows a quick capacity decay due to the severe pulverization as a result of the large volume changes of 390% during sodiation and desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> This shortcoming has hitherto stifled widespread use of Sb and other alloy anode materials in SIBs. To solve this hurdle, several strategies have been developed to construct various Sb nanostructures, such as zero-dimensional (0D) porous and yolk–shell nanoparticles, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9, 10)</a> as well as one-dimensional (1D) nanomaterials <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> to mitigate the pulverization and cracking of Sb active materials. Recently, two-dimensional (2D) materials such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> VS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> ReS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> silicene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15, 16)</a> and MXenes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a>have been studied as potential anodic materials in LIBs or SIBs, which exhibit enhanced electrochemical properties in terms of capacity, rate capability, and cycling performance compared to their bulk counterparts owing to the shorter diffusion path length for ion diffusion and enlarged surface active sites for ion storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">2D antimonene, consisting of a buckled structure of hexagonal rings, has been recently predicted <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18-20)</a> and fabricated by mechanical exfoliation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> van der Waals epitaxy, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22, 23)</a> and liquid-phase exfoliation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The large theoretical capacity of Sb and unique 2D features render few-layer antimonene (FLA) an appealing anode material for high-performance SIBs. However, the Na storage performance of 2D FLA has not been fully explored so far. Moreover, although Sb-based materials such as nanocrystals, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> nanorods, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> and nanocompostites <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> have been studied as anode materials in SIBs, the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of nanoscale Sb remains vague. In this regard, 2D FLA is ideally suited to unravel the sodiation/desodiation mechanism and maximize the Na storage performance.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we determine the superior Na storage properties of FLA and elucidate the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of FLA based on the results acquired <i>via in situ</i> synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD), <i>ex situ</i> selected-area electron diffraction (SAED), and density-functional theory (DFT) calculations. We demonstrate that FLA undergoes reversible crystalline phase evolution (Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) during cycles, and Na-ion alloying reactions proceed along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis, resulting in in-plane anisotropic lattice expansion. Since the in-plane expansion along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> axial direction of 2D FLA is not constrained, FLA could achieve high structural stability for Na ion storage. These unexpected findings differ from previous reports in which bulk Sb is believed to undergo amorphorization during cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> DFT calculations indicate that 2D FLA has a small Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier of 0.14 eV, which is much smaller than those of 2D MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> VS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and silicene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> The reversible crystalline phase transformation, anisotropic volume change, and low Na ion diffusion barrier of FLA enable excellent Na storage performances. Galvanostatic studies demonstrate that 2D FLA delivers a large charging capacity of 642 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C = 660 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and a high rate capability of 429 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high current density of 5 C and maintains a stable capacity of 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C with 99.7% capacity retention from the 10th to the 150th cycle. Considering the 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> theoretical capacity of Sb, the electrochemical utilization of Sb atoms is as high as 93.9% at a high rate of 0.5 C for over 150 cycles. They are the highest capacity and utilization ratio determined from alloy-based anodes reported so far (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information). Our study reveals the Na storage mechanism of 2D FLA and provides strategies for the design and development of 2D materials for high-energy SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The 2D FLA nanosheets are produced in a large quantity by liquid-phase exfoliation of β-antimony in 1:1 <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP)/ethanol without any surfactant (see details in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Methods">Methods</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the schematic diagram of a Na-ion half-cell, in which FLA acts as the working electrode and Na foil as the counter electrode. The atomic model of FLA is schematically shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, and the thickness of the as-synthesized FLA is measured <i>via</i> atomic force microscopy (AFM). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d are the typical AFM image and the corresponding line profile of FLA, demonstrating the thickness of FLA is about 7 nm (about 18 atomic layers). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> (Supporting Information) depicts the statistical analysis of 120 FLA flakes, showing that 78.3% FLA flakes have a thickness below 10 nm and 23.3% of flakes are thinner than 5 nm. The dimensions of as-synthesized FLA are about 200–1000 nm, as the AFM image indicates (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD pattern acquired from the FLA flakes is similar to that of the β-Sb precursor (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, Supporting Information), but the full-width at half-maximum (FWHM) values of the (003) peak increase and the peak shifts to a small angle, suggesting a thinner FLA and expanded <i>c</i>-axis interlayer distance of the FLA compared to the bulk β-Sb precursor. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28, 29)</a> The enlarged surface active sites and expanded <i>c</i>-axis interlayers of FLA enable large-capacity and fast sodiation reaction kinetics for SIBs as the following parts reveal.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic diagram of the Na-ion half-cell composed of FLA. (b) Atomic structure illustration of FLA. (c) AFM image of FLA and (d) profile terraces of FLA.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Raman scattering peaks (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, Supporting Information) acquired from FLA are weaker compared to bulk β-Sb, and a similar decreasing trend with decreasing thickness is observed from the micromechanically exfoliated Sb flakes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The A<sub>1g</sub> and E<sub>1g</sub> peaks of FLA are located at 154.0 and 116.8 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, showing blue-shifts to larger wavenumbers compared to bulk β-Sb probably because of the <i>c</i>-axis lattice expansion and in-plane lattice contraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 and 23">(21, 23)</a> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) reveals two peaks with binding energies of 537.4 and 528.0 eV, corresponding to 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of metallic Sb <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref30" aria-label="reference 10 and 30">(10, 30)</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, Supporting Information). The transmission electron microscope (TEM) image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrates that FLA is transparent, corroborating the thinner flake structure. The high-resolution TEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>, Supporting Information) discloses a single-crystalline structure of FLA with a spacing of 0.216 nm corresponding to the (110) plane of β-Sb.</div><div class="NLM_p">The 2D FLA exhibits good chemical stability, which is different from the same group 2D phosphorene that is unstable under ambient conditions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31, 32)</a> After storing at atmospheric conditions for a month, the as-synthesized 2D FLA suspension (0.5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) still exhibits a strong Tyndall effect (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, Supporting Information). X-ray energy dispersive spectrometry (EDS) confirms no obvious change in the chemical composition. The Raman peaks of A<sub>1g</sub> and E<sub>1g</sub> observed from the fresh FLA and FLA stored in atmospheric conditions for a month have no obvious change, and no additional Raman peaks of antimony oxide are observed as well (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the high chemical stability of the as-synthesized FLA. These results indicate that stable 2D FLA could be produced in a large quantity by a facile liquid-phase exfoliation approach, which is crucial to the development of 2D antimonene technology for high-energy SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of the FLA anode are evaluated using CR2025-type coin-like cells as schematically shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The long-term cycles of FLA and the bulk Sb powder precursor are measured at a current density of 0.5 C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). Bulk Sb shows a quick capacity decay due to severe pulverization as a result of the large volume changes during the sodiation and desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> However, the FLA electrode displays a stable capacity of 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C with 99.7% capacity retention from the 10th to the 150th cycle and a high Coulombic efficiency (CE) close to 100%. SEM images of FLA electrodes before and after 150 cycles are depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a,b</a> (Supporting Information), revealing no obvious morphology change. The 2D FLA could mitigate the pulverization and cracking of Sb, therefore demonstrating excellent cycle stability. Considering the theoretical capacity of 660 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Sb, the large capacity of 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C means 93.9% of the Sb atoms in FLA take part in Na storage, even after 150 cycles. They are the highest capacity and Sb utilization ratio reported so far (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information). The initial CE of FLA is 64.7%, which is smaller than that of bulk Sb (71.5%). The larger irreversible capacity loss of the FLA electrode is attributed to a higher electrolyte/electrode surface area, which causes more side reactions with the electrolyte in the first cycling process. This phenomenon is also observed from nanostructured Si and Sb electrode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref33" aria-label="reference 8 and 33">(8, 33)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b demonstrates the rate capabilities of FLA. The FLA anode delivers a large capacity of 642 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C. When the current density is increased by 50 times to 5 C, a capacity of 429 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to 67% capacity retention is achieved. When the current density is returned to 0.1 C after 35 cycles, the specific capacity recovers to 645 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, thereby demonstrating good reversibility and high rate capability. The cyclic voltammograms (CVs) of the FLA anode are depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. They are acquired at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range from 0.01 to 1.5 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). During the first cathodic scan, a strong peak at 0.35 V is observed. In the second cycle, the three reduction peaks at 0.68, 0.55, and 0.39 V are identified and subsequently stabilized at 0.68 and 0.39 V, which correspond to the multistep Na–Sb alloying reactions from Sb to the intermediate NaSb phase and finally the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In the reverse anodic scan, the two anodic peaks at 0.88 and 0.96 V are associated with the desodiation reaction from Na<sub>3</sub>Sb to NaSb and Sb, respectively. The peak current in the first cathodic scan is larger than those in the following ones due to decomposition of the electrolyte, forming solid state interface (SEI) layers on the FLA. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The CV scans overlap from the third cycle onward, indicating good reversibility and stability. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and the corresponding equivalent circuit are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c,d</a> (Supporting Information). The charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at the electrode/electrolyte interface of the FLA anode in the 150th cycle is less than that in the first cycle, implying lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> with cycles due to the slow wetting of the electrolyte and the activation of FLA electrodes, similar to Si anodes in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36, 37)</a> Electrochemical studies (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>) indicate that the 2D FLA delivers superior Na storage performance that surpasses those of previously reported Sb and alloy-based anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, Supporting Information).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Long-term cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the FLA and bulk Sb powder at a rate of 0.5 C. (b) Rate capability of FLA. (c) CV profiles of FLA between 0.01 and 1.5 V at a rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To elucidate the Na storage mechanism of FLA, the sodiation/desodiation processes of FLA are monitored by SAED, <i>in situ</i> synchrotron XRD, and DFT calculations. The <i>ex situ</i> SAED images in the sodiation states at 1.5, 0.68, and 0 V and desodiation states at 0.96 and 1.5 V in the third cycle are depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The SAED patterns at different voltages confirm the all the sodiated and desodiated samples are crystalline. The SAED patterns can be indexed to hexagonal Sb (JCPDS No. 35-0732), monoclinic NaSb (JCPDS No. 74-0801), and hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (JCPDS No. 04-0724). During the sodiation process, Na first reacts with Sb, forming crystalline NaSb at 0.68 V, and the Sb phase disappears. As sodiation continues, Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is gradually formed at a discharging voltage of 0.39 V and becomes the major species relative to NaSb at a discharging voltage of 0.01 V. During the desodiation process, the crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase gradually transforms into the crystalline NaSb intermediate phase (0.96 V) and finally the Sb phase with a small account of NaSb (1.5 V). The existence of Sb and a small NaSb phase in the full sodiation and desodiation states is attributed to incomplete sodiation/desodiation reactions at a large current density of 0.5 C. The <i>ex situ</i> SAED patterns are consistent with CV curves (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c), in which the four peaks in the CV curves are attributed to the five sequential sodiation/desodiation transformations of Sb → NaSb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb → NaSb → Sb during cycling, which are schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0007.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) <i>Ex situ</i> SAED patterns during sodiation at 1.5, 0.68, and 0.01 V and desodiation states at 0.96 and 1.5 V under a 0.5 C rate during the third discharging/charging cycle. (b) Schematic diagram showing the five states of the FLA anode during sodiation/desodiation processes corresponding to the different crystalline phases/crystal structures of Sb, NaSb, and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb at the specific charging–discharging voltage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The above results indicate that the FLA anode undergoes a reversible crystalline-phase transformation of Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb during cycling, as depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. To further reveal the lattice change in FLA during cycling, we conduct <i>in situ</i> synchrotron XRD during the third cycle as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The FLA peaks of (003) and (110) planes both shift to smaller angles during the initial sodiation processes, and then the two diffraction peaks attenuate. Meanwhile, the diffraction peaks of NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb successively appear. In the reverse desodiation process, the two peaks of NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb fade away and the Sb peaks of (003) and (110) planes enhance with the desodiation. Moreover, the FLA peaks of the (003) and (110) planes both shift to larger angles. These results clearly support the crystalline-phase transformation (Sb → NaSb → Na<sub>3</sub>Sb → NaSb → Sb) during cycling, which is consistent with <i>ex situ</i> SAED results (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). Compared to the (003) peak, the angle changes of the (110) peak are larger during the sodiation and desodiation processes, suggesting larger expansion along the (110) plane (<i>a</i>/<i>b</i> axial direction) during sodiation processes. The detailed lattice changing rates of Δ<i>d</i>(003)/<i>d</i>(003) (<i>c</i>-axis) and Δ<i>d</i>(110)/<i>d</i>(110) (<i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis) are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d. The slight increase in the <i>c</i> lattice parameter along the (003) direction during initial sodiation is attributed to Na-ion insertion between layers of FLA. The larger lattice expansion along the (110) plane (<i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis) may stem from in-plane (<i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis) alloying reactions forming crystalline NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloys. The interlayer distance of FLA is about 3.8 Å, which could accommodate the large Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Therefore, Na<sup>+</sup> insertion between the layers in FLA does not significantly alter the <i>c</i> axial lattice parameter. The inserted Na<sup><i>+</i></sup> ions further react with Sb atoms to produce crystalline NaSb and finally Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, resulting in in-plane lattice expansion along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d clearly reveal that the lattice expansion of the (110) planes is almost 3 times larger than that of the (003) plane, confirming anisotropic volume expansion along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis during sodiation reactions. For the 2D FLA, since the in-plane expansion along the <i>a</i>/b axial direction is not constrained, it can accommodate the larger anisotropic volume change along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis during the Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb transformation to achieve high structural stability for Na ion storage.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Schematic diagram of the crystalline phase evolution(Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) during the sodiation/desodiation process. (b) <i>In situ</i> XRD pattern evolution of FLA anodes during the third sodiation/desodiation process at a current of 0.2 C. (c, d) Change of Sb lattice constants of (003) and (110) planes corresponding to the <i>c</i>-axis and <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis during sodiation/desodiation processes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further evaluate the sodiation and desodiation process of FLA, we further studied Na adsorption and diffusion properties on monolayer antimonene by using first-principles calculations. Three possible adsorption sites of Na are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> (Supporting Information). The top site is above the higher Sb atom, the hollow site is above the middle of a hexagonal ring, and the valley site is above the lower Sb atom. Optimization results show that the Na adsorption is an exothermic process and the valley adsorption system is the most stable. We also explore the diffusion path and barrier between two adjacent valley sites by the complete LST/QST method. The results are illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b. The calculated energy barrier for Na diffusion is 0.14 eV, which is smaller than those of 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.28 eV), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> VS<sub>2</sub> (0.22 eV), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> silicene (0.24 eV), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> and graphene (0.22 eV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The low Na-ion diffusion barrier and slight <i>c</i>-axial lattice parameter expansion enable fast Na-ion diffusion. Furthermore, the DFT results in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c also confirm the larger lattice expansion along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis direction compared to the <i>c</i>-axis from the Sb phase to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. Our study suggests that storage of Na ions in FLA proceeds by Na-ion fast diffusion and alloying reactions forming crystalline NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloys along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis, which provide clear structural correlation with the excellent Na storage property.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-08714y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Optimal Na diffusion pathway. (b) Calculated barriers for Na-ion diffusion in FLA. (c) Calculated changes in the lattice parameters and volume during the sodiation/desodiation process (Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/asset/images/large/nn-2017-08714y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b08714&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the 2D FLA is an appealing anode material for Na storage, which shows a large capacity of 620 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 charging/discharging cycles at 0.5 C, corresponding to a 93.9% utilization ratio, and a high rate capability of 429 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 C. Storage of Na ions in FLA is believed to proceed through Na<sup>+</sup> insertion followed by in-plane alloying reactions along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis. The 2D FLA allows fast Na ion diffusion with a small diffusion barrier of 0.14 eV. We demonstrate that 2D FLA can accommodate the anisotropic volume change along the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>-axis and reversible crystalline-phase evolution (Sb ⇋ NaSb ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) during cyclings to achieve high structural stability and fast electrochemical redox kinetics for Na-ion storage. The anisotropic volume expansion, reversible crystalline phase evolution, and low Na-ion diffusion barrier of FLA enable long-life, large-capacity, and high rate-capability SIBs. Our results elucidate the Na storage mechanism of 2D FLA and provide strategies for the development of high-performance 2D materials in advanced metal-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4-1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The 2D few-layer antimonene was fabricated by the ultrasonic treatment of antimony powders in an <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidinone/ethanol mixture at 200 W for 5–6 h. Afterward, the mixture was centrifuged at 3000 rpm to remove the unexfoliated Sb, and a dark gray suspension consisting of few-layer antimonene was obtained. To obtain the FLA sample, the suspension was centrifuged at 8000 rpm, rinsed with ethanol, and then freeze-dried under vacuum. The concentration of FLA in NMP/ethanol is measured by weighing the FLA suspension and dried FLA <i>via</i> removing the NMP/ethanol solution.</div></div><div id="sec4-2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology, structure, and composition of the samples were characterized by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM, Nano SEM 450), transmission electron microscopy (Tecnai G20), energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (Oxford INCA 200), X-ray diffraction (Philips X’Pert Pro), Raman scattering (HR RamLab, 532 nm), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (AXIS-ULTRA DLD-600W). The thickness of FLA was determined by atomic force microscopy (Bruker Multimodel-8).</div></div><div id="sec4-3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrode Fabrication and Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance of FLA was evaluated using coin-like 2025 cells assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. To prepare the electrodes, the active materials, carbon black, and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) were mixed at a ratio of 65:20:15, and water was added dropwise to form a slurry. The slurry was put on a copper foil and vacuum-dried at 60 °C overnight. The mass loading of the FLA electrode was about 1.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The Na metal acted as the counter electrode, and glass fiber (Whatman) served as the separator. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as an additive.</div></div><div id="sec4-4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed on an electrochemical workstation (CHI 660E, Shanghai, China) at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 1.5 V <i>versus</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was conducted on the same instrument at frequencies between 100 kHz and 0.01 Hz with an amplitude of 5 mV. The galvanostatic charging–discharging tests were carried out on an Xinwei instrument (Shenzhen, China), and the cutoff voltage was varied from 0.01 to 1.5 V <i>versus</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The specific capacities were calculated on the basis of the mass of the active FLA, and all the electrochemical tests were performed at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec4-5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> <i>Ex Situ</i> SAED</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The coin-like 2025 cells at sodiation states of 1.5, 0.68, and 0 V and desodiation states of 0.96 and 1.5 V under a 0.5 C rate were disassembled in an argon-filled glovebox. Then, the FLA electrodes were first immersed in DEC and then in anhydrous ethanol several times. Finally, the electrodes were ultrasonically treated and the suspension of the FLA was dropped on a carbon-coated copper grid for TEM (Tecnai G20) and SAED characterizations.</div></div><div id="sec4-6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> <i>In Situ</i> Synchrotron XRD</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The <i>in situ</i> synchrotron XRD data were acquired on the Beamline BL148 (18 keV, 0.06887 nm) at the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility with an image plate detector in the transmission mode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40, 41)</a> The freestanding electrode film was composed of the active materials, carbon black, and CMC at a ratio of 65:20:15. A sodium plate as the counter electrode had a hole punched in the center for the penetrating synchrotron beam, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DEC as the electrolyte, and a Walkman glass fiber as the separator. Mylar film, stainless steel, and a sealing washer were used to assemble the <i>in situ</i> cell in an argon-filled glovebox. Discharging and charging of the cell was carried out on a Land BT2000 battery test system (Wuhan, China) between 0.01 and 1.5 V <i>versus</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a current rate of 0.2 C.</div></div><div id="sec4-7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Computational Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The theoretical derivation was carried out using DFT implemented in the DMol3 package. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 to 44">(42-44)</a> The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerholf functional was used in the GGA as implemented in the DMol3 package. The electronic eigenfunctions were expanded in terms of a localized atomic orbital basis set of double numerical plus polarization DNP quality. A 4 × 4 × 1 supercell was used to simulate Na adsorption on the monolayered antimonene. The calculation included a DFT-D correction using the Grimme method. A vacuum space of about 20 Å between antimonene layers was used to avoid any interactions. The convergence criteria of energy and force were set to 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup> Ha and 0.002 Ha/Å, and the displacement was 0.005 Ha. The atomic distance cutoff in real space was set as “Fine”, and the transition state search used complete LST/QST as a search protocol. The convergence tolerance was set to “Fine” quality, and the RMS convergence was set to 0.002 eV/Å.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsnano.7b08714</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Height profile of AFM images, thickness statistics bar chart, XRD pattern, Raman spectra, XPS spectrum, SEM, HR-TEM, EDS mapping, EIS data and the corresponding equivalent circuit, adsorption sites and Na-ion diffusion on the surface of the antimonene (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b08714/suppl_file/nn7b08714_si_001.pdf">nn7b08714_si_001.pdf (1.06 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b08714" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
